# Searching Sourcegraph

Search before you build. Existing patterns reduce tokens, ensure consistency, and surface tested solutions.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Tool Selection Logic

**Start here:**

1. **Know the exact symbol or pattern?** → `sg_keyword_search`
2. **Know the concept, not the code?** → `sg_nls_search`
3. **Need to understand how/why?** → `sg_deepsearch_read`
4. **Tracing a symbol's usage?** → `sg_find_references`
5. **Need full implementation?** → `sg_go_to_definition` → `sg_read_file`

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Exact code patterns | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Trace usage | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Understand systems | `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Read files | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
file:.*\.test\.ts$ -file:__mocks__   # Tests, exclude mocks
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

Combine filters: `repo:^github.com/myorg/backend$ file:src/handlers lang:typescript`

## Context-Aware Behaviour

**When the user provides a file path or error message:**
- Extract symbols, function names, or error codes
- Search for those exact terms first
- Trace references if the error involves a known symbol

**When the user asks "how does X work":**
- Prefer `sg_deepsearch_read` for architectural understanding
- Follow up with `sg_read_file` on key files mentioned in the response

**When the user is implementing a new feature:**
- Search for similar existing implementations first
- Read tests for usage examples
- Check for shared utilities before creating new ones

**When debugging:**
- Search for the exact error string
- Trace the symbol where the error is thrown
- Check recent changes with `sg_diff_search`

## Workflows

For detailed step-by-step workflows, see:
- `workflows/implementing-feature.md` — when building new features
- `workflows/understanding-code.md` — when exploring unfamiliar systems
- `workflows/debugging-issue.md` — when tracking down bugs

## Efficiency Rules

**Minimise tool calls:**
- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms (faster, more precise)

**Batch your understanding:**
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than reading one and asking questions
- Use `sg_deepsearch_read` for "how does X work" instead of multiple keyword searches

**Avoid common token waste:**
- Don't search all repos when you know the target repo
- Don't use `sg_deepsearch_read` for simple "find all" queries
- Don't re-read files you've already seen in this conversation

## Query Patterns

| Intent | Query |
|--------|-------|
| React hooks | `file:.*\.tsx$ use[A-Z].*= \(` |
| API routes | `file:src/api app\.(get\|post\|put\|delete)` |
| Error handling | `catch.*Error\|\.catch\(` |
| Type definitions | `file:types/ export (interface\|type)` |
| Test setup | `file:.*\.test\. beforeEach\|beforeAll` |
| Config files | `file:(webpack\|vite\|rollup)\.config` |
| CI/CD | `file:\.github/workflows deploy` |

For more patterns, see `query-patterns.md`.

## Output Formatting

**Search results:**
- Present as a brief summary, not raw tool output
- Highlight the most relevant file and line
- Include a code snippet only if it directly answers the question

**Code explanations:**
- Start with a one-sentence summary
- Use the codebase's own terminology
- Reference specific files and functions

**Recommendations:**
- Present as numbered steps if actionable
- Link to specific patterns found in the codebase
- Note any existing utilities that should be reused

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Fix |
|---------|-----|
| Searching all repos | Add `repo:^github.com/org/repo$` |
| Too many results | Add `file:` pattern or keywords |
| Missing relevant code | Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching |
| Not understanding context | Use `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Guessing patterns | Read implementations with `sg_read_file` |

## Principles

- Start narrow, expand if needed
- Chain tools: search → read → find references → definition
- Check tests for usage examples
- Read before generating

# Task: Generate API Reference Documentation for Kafka KafkaConsumer

## Objective

Generate comprehensive API reference documentation for the Apache Kafka `KafkaConsumer` Java API. The documentation should cover the complete API surface with emphasis on behavioral semantics, offset management strategies, rebalancing mechanics, and error handling patterns.

## Scope

Your documentation should cover the **KafkaConsumer API** from the `org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer` package, including:

1. **Core Consumer Lifecycle**
   - Constructor variants and configuration
   - Subscription methods (dynamic and manual assignment)
   - Polling and data fetching semantics
   - Resource cleanup and closing

2. **Offset Management**
   - Synchronous and asynchronous commit strategies
   - Offset queries and position control
   - Seek operations and offset discovery
   - Committed offset semantics

3. **Consumer Group Mechanics**
   - ConsumerRebalanceListener interface and callbacks
   - Rebalance triggers and timing
   - Group membership and heartbeat behavior
   - Partition assignment vs subscription models

4. **Flow Control and Position Management**
   - Pause and resume functionality
   - Position queries and manipulation
   - Offset-to-timestamp lookups
   - Beginning and end offset discovery

5. **Error Handling**
   - Exception types and recovery strategies
   - CommitFailedException and group fencing
   - WakeupException and thread interruption
   - Timeout and authentication errors

## Requirements

### API Methods Documentation (40%)

Document all public methods of the `KafkaConsumer` class with:
- Method signatures including all overloads
- Parameter semantics and validation rules
- Return types and their meanings
- Exception types thrown and conditions

### Behavioral Notes (30%)

Explain critical behavioral semantics:
- **poll() blocking behavior**: when it returns immediately vs when it blocks, timeout handling, rebalance callback execution during poll
- **Offset commit semantics**: difference between sync/async commits, retry behavior, commit failure handling
- **Rebalance coordination**: when rebalances occur (only during poll), callback ordering (revoked then assigned), partition ownership guarantees
- **Thread safety**: which methods are thread-safe, wakeup() special case, event loop model
- **Group membership**: max.poll.interval.ms enforcement, proactive leave behavior, session timeout vs poll timeout
- **Manual vs dynamic assignment**: mutually exclusive nature, use cases for each model
- **Position vs committed offset**: the off-by-one relationship ("committed should be next offset to read")
- **Transactional semantics**: read_committed isolation level, LSO boundary, filtered messages

### Usage Examples (20%)

Provide concrete code examples demonstrating:
- **Basic subscription and polling loop**: subscribe to topics, poll for records, process messages
- **Manual offset commit**: disable auto-commit, explicit commitSync/commitAsync after processing
- **Rebalance listener**: implement ConsumerRebalanceListener, commit offsets on revoke, initialize positions on assign
- **Seek operations**: seekToBeginning, seekToEnd, seek to specific offset, timestamp-based seeking
- **Multi-threaded processing pattern**: single consumer thread with pause/resume coordination and worker pool

### Documentation Structure (10%)

Organize documentation with clear sections:
- Overview and threading model
- Core types (KafkaConsumer, ConsumerRebalanceListener, ConsumerRecords, etc.)
- Subscription and assignment methods
- Polling and data fetching
- Offset management methods
- Flow control and position queries
- Metadata and monitoring
- Lifecycle and resource management
- Exception handling guide
- Configuration-driven behaviors
- Common patterns and best practices

## Deliverable

Write your documentation to `/workspace/documentation.md` in Markdown format.

## Notes

- Focus on **behavioral semantics** that aren't obvious from method signatures alone
- Include edge cases and gotchas (e.g., poll may block longer than timeout during rebalance callbacks)
- Explain the relationship between different offset concepts (position, committed, beginning, end, LSO)
- Cover both group-managed and standalone consumer patterns
- Document configuration properties that significantly affect API behavior
- Use the codebase to find real usage patterns in tests and internal components

## Evaluation

Your documentation will be evaluated on:
1. **Completeness**: All key API methods and types documented
2. **Accuracy**: Behavioral descriptions match actual implementation
3. **Clarity**: Complex semantics explained clearly with examples
4. **Practical value**: Real-world usage patterns and error handling strategies included

The documentation has been written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here's a summary of what was covered:

**Structure:**
1. **Overview and Threading Model** — single-threaded event loop, `wakeup()` exception, poll loop requirements
2. **Core Types** — `KafkaConsumer`, `ConsumerRecord`, `ConsumerRecords`, `OffsetAndMetadata`, `OffsetAndTimestamp`, `ConsumerGroupMetadata`
3. **Constructors** — all 4 public constructor variants with notes on deserializer behavior
4. **Subscription and Assignment** — dynamic subscription (`subscribe` with topics, Pattern, SubscriptionPattern) and manual assignment (`assign`), with mutually exclusive model explanation
5. **Polling** — full behavioral semantics of `poll()` including when it returns immediately vs blocks, rebalance callback execution, and all exceptions
6. **Offset Management** — all sync/async commit overloads, `position()`, `committed()`, and the critical "next offset to read" rule
7. **Flow Control** — `pause()`/`resume()` with rebalance caveat, `seek*` lazy evaluation, `beginningOffsets()`/`endOffsets()`/`offsetsForTimes()`, `currentLag()`
8. **Consumer Group Mechanics** — rebalance triggers, `ConsumerRebalanceListener` callbacks with ordering guarantees, eager vs cooperative rebalancing, heartbeat/session/poll interval interplay
9. **Exception Handling** — all exception types with root causes and recovery strategies
10. **Configuration Reference** — key configs and their effects
11. **Usage Examples** — 7 concrete examples covering auto-commit, manual commit, per-partition commits with `nextOffsets()`, rebalance listener with seek, timestamp-based replay, clean shutdown, and multi-threaded processing

org.apache.kafka.common.MetricName;\\n28: import org.apache.kafka.common.PartitionInfo;\\n29: import org.apache.kafka.common.TopicPartition;\\n30: import org.apache.kafka.common.Uuid;\\n31: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Time;\\n38: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Timer;\\n39: \\n40: import java.time.Duration;\\n41: import java.util.Collection;\\n42: import java.util.ConcurrentModificationException;\\n43: import java.util.List;\\n44: import java.util.Map;\\n45: import java.util.OptionalLong;\\n46: import java.util.Properties;\\n47: import java.util.Set;\\n48: import java.util.regex.Pattern;\\n49: \\n50: import static org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils.propsToMap;\\n51: \\n52: /**\\n53:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\\n54:  * <p>\\n55:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\\n56:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\\n57:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\\\"#consumergroups\\\">consumer groups</a>.\\n58:  * <p>\\n59:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\\n60:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\\n61:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\\\"#multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\\n62:  *\\n63:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\\n64:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\\n65:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\\n66:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\\n67:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\\n68:  * <p>\\n69:  *\\n70:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\\n71:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\\n72:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\\n73:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\\n74:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\\n75:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\\n76:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\\n77:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\\n78:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\\n79:  * <p>\\n80:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\\n81:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\\n82:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\\n83:  * <p>\\n84:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\\n85:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\\n86:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\\n87:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\\n88:  * <p>\\n89:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\\n90:  * detail below.\\n91:  *\\n92:  * <h3><a name=\\\"consumergroups\\\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\\n93:  *\\n94:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\\n95:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\\n96:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\\n97:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\\n98:  * <p>\\n99:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\\n100:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\\n101:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\\n102:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\\n103:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\\n104:  * <p>\\n105:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\\n106:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\\n107:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\\n108:  * detail <a href=\\\"#failuredetection\\\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\\n109:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\\n110:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\\n111:  * assign them to members of the group.\\n112:  * <p>\\n113:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\\n114:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\\n115:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\\n116:  * <p>\\n117:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\\n118:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\\n119:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\\n120:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\\n121:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\\n122:  * <p>\\n123:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\\n124:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\\n125:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\\\"#rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\\n126:  * <p>\\n127:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\\\"#manualassignment\\\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\\n128:  * (similar to the older \\\"simple\\\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\\n129:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\\n130:  *\\n131:  * <h3><a name=\\\"failuredetection\\\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\\n132:  *\\n133:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\\n134:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\\n135:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\\n136:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\\n137:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\\n138:  * be reassigned.\\n139:  * <p>\\n140:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \\\"livelock\\\" situation where it is continuing\\n141:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\\n142:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\\n143:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\\n144:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\\n145:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\\n146:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\\n147:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\\n148:  * <p>\\n149:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\\n150:  * <ol>\\n151:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\\n152:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\\n153:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\\n154:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\\n155:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\\n156:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\\n157:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\\n158:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\\n159:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\\n160:  * </ol>\\n161:  * <p>\\n162:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\\n163:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\\n164:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\\n165:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\\n166:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\\n167:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\\n168:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\\n169:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\\n170:  *\\n171:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\\n172:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\\n173:  * demonstrate how to use them.\\n174:  *\\n175:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\\n176:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\\n177:  * <p>\\n178:  * <pre>\\n179:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n180:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n181:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n182:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;true&quot;);\\n183:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;auto.commit.interval.ms&quot;, &quot;1000&quot;);\\n184:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n185:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n186:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n187:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n188:  *     while (true) {\\n189:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n190:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records)\\n191:  *             System.out.printf(&quot;offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n&quot;, record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\\n192:  *     }\\n193:  * </pre>\\n194:  *\\n195:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\\n196:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\\n197:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\\n198:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n199:  * <p>\\n200:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\\n201: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-benchmarks/kafka--be816b82@7bb066906e7979c9d10cdc924186dfe3f49a9428/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL1-200\"}",

import java.util.regex.Pattern;\n49: \n50: import static org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils.propsToMap;\n51: \n52: /**\n53:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\n54:  * <p>\n55:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\n56:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\n57:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\"#consumergroups\">consumer groups</a>.\n58:  * <p>\n59:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\n60:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\n61:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\"#multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\n62:  *\n63:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\n64:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\n65:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\n66:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\n67:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\n68:  * <p>\n69:  *\n70:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n71:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n72:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n73:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\n74:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\n75:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\n76:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\n77:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\n78:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n79:  * <p>\n80:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n81:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n82:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n83:  * <p>\n84:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n85:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n86:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n87:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n88:  * <p>\n89:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\n90:  * detail below.\n91:  *\n92:  * <h3><a name=\"consumergroups\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\n93:  *\n94:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\n95:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\n96:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\n97:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\n98:  * <p>\n99:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n100:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n101:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n102:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n103:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n104:  * <p>\n105:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n106:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n107:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n108:  * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n109:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n110:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n111:  * assign them to members of the group.\n112:  * <p>\n113:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n114:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n115:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n116:  * <p>\n117:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n118:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n119:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n120:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n121:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n122:  * <p>\n123:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n124:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n125:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n126:  * <p>\n127:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n128:  * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n129:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n130:  *\n131:  * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n132:  *\n133:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n134:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n135:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n136:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n137:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n138:  * be reassigned.\n139:  * <p>\n140:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n141:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n142:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n143:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n144:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n145:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n146:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n147:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n148:  * <p>\n149:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n150:  * <ol>\n151:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n152:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n153:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n154:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n155:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n156:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n157:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n158:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n159:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n160:  * </ol>\n161:  * <p>\n162:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n163:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n164:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n165:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n166:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n167:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n168:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n169:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n170:  *\n171:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n172:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n173:  * demonstrate how to use them.\n174:  *\n175:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n176:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n177:  * <p>\n178:  * <pre>\n179:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n180:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\n181:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\n182:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;true&quot;);\n183:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;auto.commit.interval.ms&quot;, &quot;1000&quot;);\n184:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n185:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n186:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\n187:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\n188:  *     while (true) {\n189:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n190:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records)\n191:  *             System.out.printf(&quot;offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n&quot;, record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n192:  *     }\n193:  * </pre>\n194:  *\n195:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n196:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. See the NOTICE file distributed with\\n4:  * this work for additional information regarding copyright ownership.\\n5:  * The ASF licenses this file to You under the Apache License, Version 2.0\\n6:  * (the \\\"License\\\"); you may not use this file except in compliance with\\n7:  * the License. You may obtain a copy of the License at\\n8:  *\\n9:  *    http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\\n10:  *\\n11:  * Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\\n12:  * distributed under the License is distributed on an \\\"AS IS\\\" BASIS,\\n13:  * WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\\n14:  * See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\\n15:  * limitations under the License.\\n16:  */\\n17: package org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer;\\n18: \\n19: import org.apache.kafka.common.TopicPartition;\\n20: \\n21: import java.time.Duration;\\n22: import java.util.Collection;\\n23: \\n24: /**\\n25:  * A callback interface that the user can implement to trigger custom actions when the set of partitions assigned to the\\n26:  * consumer changes.\\n27:  * <p>\\n28:  * This is applicable when the consumer is having Kafka auto-manage group membership. If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\\n29:  * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\\n30:  * <p>\\n31:  * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\\n32:  * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\\n33:  * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\\n34:  * administratively adjusted).\\n35:  * <p>\\n36:  * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\\n37:  * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\\n38:  * the offset gets saved.\\n39:  * <p>\\n40:  * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\\n41:  * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\\n42:  * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\\n43:  * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\\n44:  * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\\n45:  * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\\n46:  * consumption.\\n47:  * <p>\\n48:  * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\\n49:  * whenever partition assignment changes.\\n50:  * <p>\\n51:  * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\\n52:  * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\\n53:  * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\\n54:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will be always accessible by the time the\\n55:  * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\\n56:  * <p>\\n57:  * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\\n58:  * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\\n59:  * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\\n60:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\\n61:  * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\\n62:  * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\\n63:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\\n64:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\\n65:  * at that time.\\n66:  * <p>\\n67:  * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\\n68:  * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\\n69:  * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\\n70:  * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\\n71:  * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\\n72:  * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\\n73:  * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\\n74:  * <p>\\n75:  * It is possible\\n76:  * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\\n77:  * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\\n78:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\\n79:  * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\\n80:  * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an exception, this exception will be propagated to the current\\n81:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} as well.\\n82:  * <p>\\n83:  * Note that callbacks only serve as way,\\n86:  * as it will be propagated all the way up to the {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} call.\\n87:  * If user captures the exception in the caller, the callback is still assumed successful and no further retries will be attempted.\\n88:  * <p>\\n89:  *\\n90:  * Here is pseudo-code for a callback implementation for saving offsets:\\n91:  * <pre>\\n92:  * {@code\\n93:  * If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\n29:  * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\n30:  * <p>\n31:  * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\n32:  * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\n33:  * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\n34:  * administratively adjusted).\n35:  * <p>\n36:  * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\n37:  * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\n38:  * the offset gets saved.\n39:  * <p>\n40:  * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\n41:  * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\n42:  * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\n43:  * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\n44:  * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\n45:  * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\n46:  * consumption.\n47:  * <p>\n48:  * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\n49:  * whenever partition assignment changes.\n50:  * <p>\n51:  * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\n52:  * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\n53:  * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\n54:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will be always accessible by the time the\n55:  * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\n56:  * <p>\n57:  * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\n58:  * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\n59:  * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\n60:  * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\n61:  * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\n62:  * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\n63:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\n64:  * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\n65:  * at that time.\n66:  * <p>\n67:  * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\n68:  * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\n69:  * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\n70:  * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n71:  * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\n72:  * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n73:  * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\n74:  * <p>\n75:  * It is possible\n76:  * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\n77:  * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\n78:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\n79:  * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\n80:  * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an exception, this exception will be propagated to the current\n81:  * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} as well.\n82:  * <p>\n83:  * Note that callbacks only serve as way,\n86:  * as it will be propagated all the way up to the {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} call.\n87:  * If user captures the exception in the caller, the callback is still assumed   "content": "{\"content\":\"200:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\\n201:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\\n202:  * <p>\\n203:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\\n204:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\\n205:  * <p>\\n206:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\\n207:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\\n208:  *\\n209:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\\n210:  *\\n211:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\\n212:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\\n213:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\\n214: \\n215:  * <p>\\n216:  * <pre>\\n217:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n218:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n219:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n220:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;false&quot;);\\n221:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n222:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n223:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n224:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n225:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\\n226:  *     List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; buffer = new ArrayList&lt;&gt;();\\n227:  *     while (true) {\\n228:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n229:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records) {\\n230:  *             buffer.add(record);\\n231:  *         }\\n232:  *         if (buffer.size() &gt;= minBatchSize) {\\n233:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\\n234:  *             consumer.commitSync();\\n235:  *             buffer.clear();\\n236:  *         }\\n237:  *     }\\n238:  * </pre>\\n239:  *\\n240:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\\n241:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\\n242:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\\n243:  * possible\\n244:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\\n245:  * <p>\\n246:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\\n247:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\\n248:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\\n249:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\\n250:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\\n251:  * Kafka provides what is often called \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\\n252:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\\n253:  * <p>\\n254:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery, but the requirement is that\\n255:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\\n256:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\\n257:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\\n258:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \\\"consumed.\\\"</b>\\n259:  * <p>\\n260:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\\n261:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\\n262:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\\n263:  * <p>\\n264:  * <pre>\\n265:  *     try {\\n266:  *         while(running) {\\n267:  *             ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\\n268:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\\n269:  *                 List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\\n270:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : partitionRecords) {\\n271:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + &quot;: &quot; + record.value());\\n272:  *                 }\\n273:  *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\\n274:  *             }\\n275:  *         }\\n276:  *     } finally {\\n277:  *       consumer.close();\\n278:  *     }\\n279:  * </pre>\\n280:  *\\n281:  * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\\n282:  * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\\n283:  * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\\n284:  * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\\n285:  * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\\n286:  *\\n287:  * <h4><a name=\\\"manualassignment\\\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\\n288:  *\\n289:  * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\\n290:  * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\\n291:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\\n292:  * <p>\\n293:  * <ul>\\n294:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\\n295:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\\n296:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\\n297:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\\n298:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\\n299:  * will be restarted on another machine.\\n300:  * </ul>\\n301:  * <p>\\n302:  * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\\n303:  * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\\n304:  *\\n305:  * <pre>\\n306:  *     String topic = &quot;foo&quot;;\\n307:  *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\\n308:  *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\\n309:  *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\\n310:  * </pre>\\n311:  *\\n312:  * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\\n313:  * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\\n314:  * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\\n315:  * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\\n316:  * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\\n317:  * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\\n318:  * <p>\\n319:  * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\\n320:  * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\\n321:  *\\n322:  * <h4><a name=\\\"rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\\n323:  *\\n324:  * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\\n325:  * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\\n326:  * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\\n327:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \\\"exactly once\\\" semantics that are\\n328:  * stronger than the default \\\"at-least once\\\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\\n329:  * <p>\\n330:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\\n331:  * <ul>\\n332:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\\n333:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\\n334:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\\n335:  * won't be updated.\\n336:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\\n337:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\\n338:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\\n339:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\\n340:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\\n341:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\\n342:  * </ul>\\n343:  * <p>\\n344:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\\n345:  *\\n346:  * <ul>\\n347:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\\n348:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\\n349:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\\n350:  * </ul>\\n351:  *\\n352:  * <p>\\n353:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\\n354:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\\n355:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\\n356:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\\n357:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\\n358:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\\n359:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\\n360:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\\n361:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\\n362:  * <p>\\n363:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\\n364:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\\n365:  *\\n366:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\\n367:  *\\n368:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\\n369:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\\n370:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\\n371:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\\n372:  * <p>\\n373:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\\n374:  * <p>\\n375:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\\n376:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\\n377:  * <p>\\n378:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\\n379:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\\n380:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\\n381:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\\n382:  * <p>\\n383:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\\n384:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\\n385:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\\n386:  *\\n387:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\\n388:  *\\n389:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\\n390:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\\n391:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\\n392:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\\n393:  *\\n394:  * <p>\\n395:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\\n396:  * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\\n397:  * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstrapping upon consumer starting up where there are\\n398:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\\n399:  * fetching other topics.\\n400:  *\\n401:  * <p>\\n402:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\\n403:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\\n404:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\\n405:  *\\n406:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\\n407:  *\\n408:  * <p>\\n409:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\\n410:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\\n411:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\\n412:  *\\n413:  * <p>\\n414:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\\n415:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\\n416:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\\n417:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\\n418:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\\n419:  *\\n420:  * <p>\\n421:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\\n422:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\\n423:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\\n424:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\\n425:  *\\n426:  * <p>\\n427:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\\n428:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\\n429:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\\n430:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\\n431:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\\n432:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\\n433:  *\\n434:  * <h3><a name=\\\"multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\\n435:  *\\n436:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\\n437:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\\n438:  *\\n439:  * <p>\\n440:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\\n441:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\\n442:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\\n443:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\\n444:  *\\n445:  * <pre>\\n446:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\\n447:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\\n448:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\\n449:  *\\n450:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\\n451:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\\n452:  *     }\\n453:  *\\n454:  *     {@literal}@Override\\n455:  *     public void run() {\\n456:  *         try {\\n457:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\\\"topic\\\"));\\n458:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\\n459:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\\n460:  *                 // Handle new records\\n461:  *             }\\n462:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\\n463:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\\n464:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\\n465:  *         } finally {\\n466:  *             consumer.close();\\n467:  *         }\\n468:  *     }\\n469:  *\\n470:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\\n471:  *     public void shutdown() {\\n472:  *         closed.set(true);\\n473:  *         consumer.wakeup();\\n474:  *     }\\n475:  * }\\n476:  * </pre>\\n477:  *\\n478:  * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\\n479:  *\\n480:  * <p>\\n481:  * <pre>\\n482:  *     closed.set(true);\\n483:  *     consumer.wakeup();\\n484:  * </pre>\\n485:  *\\n486:  * <p>\\n487:  * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\\n488:  * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\\n489:  * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\\n490:  * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\\n491:  *\\n492:  * <p>\\n493:  * We have intentionally avoided implementing a particular threading model for processing. This leaves several\\n494:  * options for implementing multi-threaded processing of records.\\n495:  *\\n496:  * <h4>1. One Consumer Per Thread</h4>\\n497:  *\\n498:  * A simple option is to give each thread its own consumer instance. Here are the pros and cons of this approach:\\n499:  * <ul>\\n500:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is the easiest to implement\\n501: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-benchmarks/kafka--be816b82@7bb066906e7979c9d10cdc924186dfe3f49a9428/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL200-500\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "200:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n201:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n202:  * <p>\n203:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n204:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n205:  * <p>\n206:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n207:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n208:  *\n209:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n210:  *\n211:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n212:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n213:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n214: \n215:  * <p>\n216:  * <pre>\n217:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n218:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\n219:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\n220:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;false&quot;);\n221:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n222:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\n223:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\n224:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\n225:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n226:  *     List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; buffer = new ArrayList&lt;&gt;();\n227:  *     while (true) {\n228:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n229:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records) {\n230:  *             buffer.add(record);\n231:  *         }\n232:  *         if (buffer.size() &gt;= minBatchSize) {\n233:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n234:  *             consumer.commitSync();\n235:  *             buffer.clear();\n236:  *         }\n237:  *     }\n238:  * </pre>\n239:  *\n240:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. However, in\n291:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n292:  * <p>\n293:  * <ul>\n294:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n295:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\n296:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\n297:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework).
